ECK125269256	ECK120011383	Lrp	Lrp, "<b>L</b>eucine-responsive <b>r</b>egulatory <b>p</b>rotein," is a dual transcriptional regulator for at least 10% of the genes in <i>Escherichia coli</i> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Tani TH, Khodursky A, Blumenthal RM, Brown PO, Matthews RG,2002</a>. These genes are involved in amino acid biosynthesis and catabolism, nutrient transport, pili synthesis, and other cellular functions, including 1-carbon metabolism <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Ernsting BR,1992. Brinkman AB,2003. Calvo JM,1994</a>. In addition, Lrp affects nearly three-fourths of the genes induced upon entry into stationary phase <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Tani TH, Khodursky A, Blumenthal RM, Brown PO, Matthews RG,2002. Cho BK, Barrett CL, Knight EM, Park YS, Palsson BØ,2008</a>. Lrp might also play a topological role in dynamic DNA packaging <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">D'Ari R, Lin RT, Newman EB,1993</a>.  Lrp can act as a repressor or activator for its target genes. Binding of leucine can affect these activities in three different ways: leucine either potentiates, antagonizes, or has no discernible effect upon Lrp action <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lin R,1992. Platko JV,1993. Newman EB, Lin R,1995</a>. It is believed that Lrp senses the presence of rich nutrition based on the concentration of leucine and positively regulates genes that function during famine and negatively regulates genes that function during a feast <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Calvo JM,1994</a>.  Lrp forms a mixture of octamers and hexadecamers <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chen S, Rosner MH, Calvo JM,2001</a>. In the presence of leucine, the octamer configuration is favored. It is believed that the switch between the octameric and hexadecameric states modulated by leucine affects the binding and the activity of Lrp at its target genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chen S, Calvo JM,2002</a>. The structure of Lrp from <i>Escherichia coli</i> bound to DNA as an octamer has been solved <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">de los Rios S, Perona JJ,2007</a>. Lrp forms an open ring structure, in which the DNA is wrapped around the octamer in a nucleosome-like structure. The monomer chain contains two domains, an N-terminal helix-turn-helix motif and a C-terminal &alpha;&beta;-sandwich fold.   The N-terminal is responsible for the specificity and function among members of the Lrp regulon across the <i>Enterobacteriaceae</i>. The N-terminal tail plays a significant role in modulating Lrp function, similar to what has been reported for a number of other transcriptional regulators <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hart BR, Mishra PK, Lintner RE, Hinerman JM, Herr AB, Blumenthal RM,2011</a>.  N-terminal diversity is responsible for some of the differences between Lrp TF orthologs in terms of DNA binding and multimerization <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hart BR, Mishra PK, Lintner RE, Hinerman JM, Herr AB, Blumenthal RM,2011</a>.  Lrp-regulated promoters commonly contain multiple adjacent binding sites for the protein with low sequence specificity. A consensus recognition sequence has been described <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Cui Y,1996</a>. The identified 15-bp sequence motif is structured with flanking CAG/CTG triplets and a central AT-rich signal. Leucine has two effects upon binding to DNA <i>in vitro</i>: the affinity of Lrp is reduced but the cooperativity of binding to multiple sites is increased <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chen S, Iannolo M, Calvo JM,2005</a>.  Lrp belongs to the Lrp/AsnC family of transcriptional regulatory proteins, which is widely distributed throughout the eubacterial and archaeal domains. Lrp appears to be very well conserved in members of the &gamma;-proteobacteria <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Brinkman AB,2003</a>. Based on phylogenetic and Logo analysis, a conserved signature in the Lrp TF and its paralog, AscN, were identified in &gamma;-proteobacteria <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Unoarumhi Y, Blumenthal RM, Matson JS,2016</a>. Likewise, specific residues that correlated with global versus local regulatory roles were determined <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Unoarumhi Y, Blumenthal RM, Matson JS,2016</a>.  A microarray analysis for Lrp of <i>Escherichia coli</i> was done by <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hung SP, Baldi P, Hatfield GW,2002</a>.  Based on whole-proteome analysis, three lysines (K28, K39, and K132) were identified as points of acetylation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Baeza J,2014</a>.			ECK12	Active

Hopper S, Böck A,1995</a> the central domain, which is probably involved in open complex formation, ATP hydrolysis, and interaction with &sigma;<sup>54</sup> and formate <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Weiss DS, Batut J, Klose KE, Keener J, Kustu S,1991. However, alignments made by the curators of this database showed that the sequence is less conserved in the upstream regions regulated. This result also showed that the motif TGTCGnnnnTGACA is conserved in the sequences examined, and for this reason the binding sites for FhlA regulation have been relocated, reassigned, and corrected.  The consensus sequence TGTCGnnnnTGACA is in agreement with alignments determined by the curator for these upstream regions and with the corresponding evidence obtained in the bibliography of every operon, including similarity to the consensus sequence and the data from footprinting assays, computational analysis, and profiling of FhlA-dependent gene expression <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hopper S,1994. Self WT,2004</a>. The FhlA-binding sites are represented by an inverted 14-bp repeat motif.  Review: <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Repoila F, Majdalani N, Gottesman S,2003</a>. ECK125269258	ECK120011306	PhoB-Phosphorylated	PhoB is a dual transcription regulator that activates expression of the Pho regulon in response to environmental P<sub>i</sub>. Under phosphate limitation conditions the inner membrane sensor kinase PhoR autophosphorylates. Subsequent transfer of the phosphate group to PhoB results in activation of PhoB <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Makino K, Shinagawa H, Amemura M, Kawamoto T, Yamada M, Nakata A,1989</a> When phosphate is in excess, autophosphorylation of PhoR is inhibited and PhoB-P is dephosphorylated. site <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">21829166</a> The output domain belongs to the winged helix-turn-helix family of transcription factors <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martínez-Hackert E, Stock AM,1997</a> Its activity is silenced by the receiver domain, and phosphorylation relieves inhibition <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Ellison DW, McCleary WR,2000</a> The 3D structure has been solved class="linkBlue">Arribas-Bosacoma R, Kim SK, Ferrer-Orta C, Blanco AG, Pereira PJ, Gomis-Rüth FX, Wanner BL, Coll M, Solà M,2007</a> as well as that for the output domain <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martínez-Hackert E, Stock AM,1997</a> Aspartic acid 196 (D196) and arginine 219 (R219), located in the C-terminal DNA-binding domain minor groove, are necessary for the binding process for the PhoB transcriptional regulator <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Ritzefeld M, Wollschläger K, Niemann G, Anselmetti D, Sewald N,2011</a> Based on biochemical analysis, a positive cooperative binding mechanism of PhoB<sup>DBD</sup> (DNA-binding domain) to its cognate DNA sequence and a decisive contribution of dimerization to the complex stability were demonstrated <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">24199636</a>  PhoB binds to the Pho box, which has been described as two direct 11-bp repeats consisting of successive 7-bp direct repeats followed by an A/T-rich region Makino K,1986</a> The <i>pho</i> promoters contain functional  -10 sequences but lack the consensus  -35 sequence <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kimura S,1989</a>  The 3D structures of the receiver domain in the absence and presence of the phosphoryl analog beryllium fluoride <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Bachhawat P, Swapna GV, Montelione GT, Stock AM,2005. Solá M, Gomis-Rüth FX, Serrano L, González A, Coll M,1999</a> and of two constitutively active mutants of this domain <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Arribas-Bosacoma R, Kim SK, Ferrer-Orta C, Blanco AG, Pereira PJ, Gomis-Rüth FX, Wanner BL, Coll M, Solà M,2007</a> have been solved. The data reveal that the active and inactive receiver domains dimerize in two different orientations relative to each other. Also, the 3D structures of the output domain without DNA <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">10653699</a>and in complex with its target DNA have been solved <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Blanco AG, Sola M, Gomis-Rüth FX, Coll M,2002</a> These data reveal that the output domains bind in a novel tandem arrangement, in which several monomers bind head to tail to successive 11-bp direct repeat sequences, coating one face of the double helix <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Blanco AG, Sola M, Gomis-Rüth FX, Coll M,2002</a> In addition, a hybrid protein, in which the receiver domain has been replaced by a leucine zipper, is active <i>in vivo</i> as a transcriptional activator <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">19371748</a>   Taken together these data suggest that PhoB is activated through an intermolecular mechanism. In unphosphorylated PhoB the output domains are constrained. Phosphorylation changes the relative orientation of the subunits to each other, such that orientational constraints between the output domains are lost, allowing the tandem head-to-tail orientation for the active conformation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Bachhawat P, Swapna GV, Montelione GT, Stock AM,2005. 19371748. ECK125269259	ECK120013010	AsnC	AsnC is a transcriptional regulator that activates the expression of <i>asnA</i>, a gene involved in the synthesis of asparagine <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kolling R,1985. de Wind N, de Jong M, Meijer M, Stuitje AR,1985</a> However, when asparagine is present the activation of <i>asnA</i> is turned off. On the other hand, AsnC is negatively autoregulated, but the presence of asparagine does not affect this regulation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kolling R,1985</a>  AsnC belongs to the AsnC family of regulatory proteins whose members are widely distributed in archaea and bacteria <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Pérez-Rueda E, Collado-Vides J,2001</a> It has been postulated that AsnC is very ascient; it appears that it was present before the divergence of archea and bacteria <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Pérez-Rueda E, Collado-Vides J,2001</a> This protein has a DNA-binding domain with a helix-turn-helix motif in the N terminus and an effector-binding C-terminal domain <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Thaw P,2006</a>  A crystal structure of AsnC complexed with the ligand l-asparagine has been solved at 2.4-&Aring; resolution. The crystal structure shows an octamer with l-asparagine bound in a cleft at the interface between dimers; however, the octamer is formed in an l-asparagine-independent manner <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Thaw P,2006</a> The octamer is composed of four dimers, each of which appears to recognize and bind an inverted repeat DNA sequence of 5 bp separated by 3 bp that are usually T or A base pairs. There are four AsnC sites in the divergent regulatory regions of <i>asnA</i> and <i>asnC</i>, and they are separated by 18 bp <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Thaw P,2006. Suzuki03</a>  The replication origin, <i>oriC</i>, is located between the <i>mioC</i> and <i>gidA</i> genes, and the <i>asnC</i> gene is immediately upstream of <i>mioC</i> in the genome <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kolling R,1985</a> The transcription of <i>asnC</i> sometimes traverses the origin of replication and continues into <i>gidA</i>, possibly explaining the effect on <i>gidA</i> expression <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kolling R,1988. Gielow A,1988. Schaefer C, Messer W,1988</a> Sometimes the transcription of <i>asnC</i> ends at a terminator located downstream of this gene or by the DnaA protein that binds to a DnaA box blocking the transcribing RNA polymerase <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gielow A,1988. Schaefer C, Messer W,1988</a>  Expression of <i>asnC</i> is regulated by nitrogen availability; under nitrogen-limiting conditions, Nac represses <i>asnC</i> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Poggio S,2002</a>  Review: <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yokoyama K, Ishijima SA, Clowney L, Koike H, Aramaki H, Tanaka C, Makino K, Suzuki M,2006</a>			ECK12	Active	Apo
ECK125269260	ECK120011229	FNR	FNR is the primary transcriptional regulator that mediates the transition from aerobic to anaerobic growth through the regulation of hundreds of genes. Generally, this protein activates genes involved in anaerobic metabolism and represses genes involved in aerobic metabolism <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Salmon K,2003. Kang Y,2005</a>. FNR also regulates the transcription of many genes with other functions, such as acid resistance, chemotaxis, cell structure, and molecular biosynthesis, among others <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Salmon K,2003. Kang Y,2005</a>.  The cellular concentration of FNR is similar under both anaerobic and aerobic growth <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Sutton VR, Mettert EL, Beinert H, Kiley PJ,2004</a>, but its activity is regulated directly by oxygen. Under anaerobiosis, FNR acquires a [4Fe-4S] cluster that causes a conformational change and dimerization of  the protein that causes it to become activated <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Moore LJ, Kiley PJ,2001</a>. Purification of [4Fe-4S]-FNR in an O<sub>2</sub>-free environment has been described <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yan A, Kiley PJ,2009</a>. The presence of O<sub>2</sub> results in inactivation of FNR via oxidation of this [4Fe-4S] cluster into a [2Fe-2S] cluster <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Jervis AJ, Crack JC, White G, Artymiuk PJ, Cheesman MR, Thomson AJ, Le Brun NE, Green J,2009. Sutton VR, Mettert EL, Beinert H, Kiley PJ,2004. Khoroshilova N, Popescu C, Münck E, Beinert H, Kiley PJ,1997</a> and the disassembly of the dimer <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lazazzera BA, Beinert H, Khoroshilova N, Kennedy MC, Kiley PJ,1996</a>. After prolonged O<sub>2</sub> exposure, the [2Fe-2S] cluster is destroyed, and apo-FNR, which lacks an Fe-S cluster, is the primary form of FNR under aerobiosis <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Reinhart F, Achebach S, Koch T, Unden G,2008. Sutton VR, Stubna A, Patschkowski T, Münck E, Beinert H, Kiley PJ,2004</a>. Nitric oxide (NO) is also able to inactivate FNR through the nitrosylation of the [4Fe-4S] cluster, although NO is sensed by other transcription factors, and FNR appears only to function as a final safeguard against NO damage <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Crack JC, Stapleton MR, Green J, Thomson AJ, Le Brun NE,2013. Cruz-Ramos H,2002</a>. The phylogenetic relationship of the [4Fe-4S]-containing O<sub>2</sub> sensor was analyzed in bacteria <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Barth C, Weiss MC, Roettger M, Martin WF, Unden G,2018</a>  Based on mass spectrometry, intermediates between the O<sub>2</sub>-driven [4Fe-4S] to [2Fe-2S] conversion, such as [3Fe-3S] and [2Fe-2S](S)<sub>n</sub>, have been identified <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Crack JC, Thomson AJ, Le Brun NE,2017</a>. Kinetic data showed that cluster sulfide oxidation occurred in parallel with cluster conversion <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Crack JC, Thomson AJ, Le Brun NE,2017</a>.  Under aerobiosis, the apo-FNR monomer is exposed and can be degraded by the ClpXP protease <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Mettert EL, Kiley PJ,2005</a>. Two motifs of FNR appear to be necessary for this degradation, one located  in the N-terminal region and the other in the C-terminal region <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Mettert EL, Kiley PJ,2005</a>.  The activated FNR conformation is able to bind a specific palindromic sequence of DNA  with the consensus sequence TTGATNNNNATCAA <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gerasimova AV,2001. Eiglmeier K,1989</a>. The G and the first T of each FNR half-site appears to interact with FNR residues Glu-209 and Ser-212 <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Spiro S, Gaston KL, Bell AI, Roberts RE, Busby SJ, Guest JR,1990</a>. FNR activates the transcription from class I and class II promoters, in which the FNR-binding sites are located  around -61, -71, -82, or  -92 in class I  and around -41.5 in class II <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wing HJ, Williams SM, Busby SJ,1995</a>.  In class I promoters the second N and in class II promoters the third N of the consensus FNR site tend to be an A-T <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Scott C, Partridge JD, Stephenson JR, Green J,2003</a>.  During activation, three activating regions (AR) are surface exposed for contact with the polymerase and promote transcription. The AR1 region contacts with the &alpha; subunit <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lee DJ, Wing HJ, Savery NJ, Busby SJ,2000</a>, the AR2 region makes contact with the &sigma;<sup>70</sup> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Blake T, Barnard A, Busby SJ, Green J,2002</a>, and the AR3 contacts the &alpha; NTD of the RNA polymerase <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lamberg KE, Luther C, Weber KD, Kiley PJ,2002</a>. The group amino acids Thr-118 with Ser-187; Lys-49 with Lys50; and Asp86 and Ile81 with Gly-85 are important for AR1, AR2, and AR3, respectively, to make contact with the RNA polymerase <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lee DJ, Wing HJ, Savery NJ, Busby SJ,2000. Blake T, Barnard A, Busby SJ, Green J,2002. Lamberg KE, Luther C, Weber KD, Kiley PJ,2002</a>. The monomer in the FNR dimer that makes contact with the RNA polymerase and the activating region in FNR that makes the contact depends on the class of promoter, class I or class II <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lee DJ, Wing HJ, Savery NJ, Busby SJ,2000. Blake T, Barnard A, Busby SJ, Green J,2002. Lamberg KE, Luther C, Weber KD, Kiley PJ,2002</a>.  FNR belongs to the CRP/FNR superfamily of transcription factors whose members are widely distributed in bacteria <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Korner H,2003</a>.  These proteins have an N-terminal sensory domain, a C-terminal helix-turn-helix DNA-binding domain, and a dimerization motif in between <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Korner H,2003</a>. The sensory domain of FNR contains five cysteine residues, four of which are essential for linking the [4Fe-4S] cluster <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Green J, Sharrocks AD, Green B, Geisow M, Guest JR,1993</a>.   The intrinsic curvature and flexibility of binding sites of FNR were investigated. FNR showed higher levels of intrinsic curvature than the NAPs, such as IHF, H-NS, Fis, and Dps, and this curvature of binding sites is independent of the sequence-level conservation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Liu G, Ma Q, Xu Y,2018</a> The binding sites for FNR have high intrinsic curvature compared with flanking regions, and these are characterized by high structural flexibility  <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Liu G, Ma Q, Xu Y,2018</a>  Under anaerobic growth conditions, transcription of the <i>fnr</i> gene is negatively autoregulated <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Mettert EL,2007</a>. The growth and the net final biomass produced in an <i>fnr</i> single deletion mutant strain growing under anaerobic fermentation conditions were affected relative to responses of the wild-type strain (<i>Escherichia coli</i> strain BW 25113) grown in the same conditions <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kargeti M, Venkatesh KV,2017</a>  FNR was named for the mutant defect in "<b>f</b>umarate and <b>n</b>itrate <b>r</b>eduction" <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lambden PR,1976</a>.  <i>fnr</i> mutants, also known as type III mutants, had originally been given the locus name <i>frdB</i> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Bachmann BJ, Low KB, Taylor AL,1976</a>.  A new variant of FNR, (FNRD154A)<sub>2</sub>, was used to identify FNR interactions on the <i>ydfZ</i> promoter region <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shan Y,2012</a>.  (FNRD154A)<sub>2</sub> also recognizes an FNR-dependent promoter under aerobic conditions and maintains its promoter specificity and conformational stability.  This variant was designed with enhanced stability to O<sub>2</sub>.  It could have a major impact on <i>in vitro</i> verification of the vast numbers of previously unrecognized genes in the FNR regulon, especially in genomic studies <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shan Y,2012</a>.  Deletion of global transcription regulators such as Fnr, ArcA, IHF, CRP, and Fis under anaerobic fermentation conditions increased the glucose uptake rate above that required for the observed growth rate. In addition, higher ATP synthesis and redox balance rates were reported relative to the observed growth rate and biomass yield <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kargeti M, Venkatesh KV,2018</a>  Using  information available in EcoCyc and RegulonDB, 393 CRP feed-forward loops (FFLs) were identified. Microarray analyses revealed dynamic gene expression of each target in response to a range of cAMP dosages; eight types of FFLs were identified, and they were divided into five functional groups <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yang CD, Huang HY, Shrestha S, Chen YH, Huang HD, Tseng CP,2018</a>  Reviews: <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Crack JC, Le Brun NE, Thomson AJ, Green J, Jervis AJ,2008. Korner H,2003. Kiley PJ,1998. Unden G,2002. Green J, Crack JC, Thomson AJ, LeBrun NE,2009</a>			ECK12	Active	
ECK125269261	ECK120013191	MarR				ECK12	Active	Apo
ECK125269262	ECK120012854	PurR-hypoxanthine		Allosteric		ECK12	Active	Holo
ECK125269263	ECK120011294	H-NS	The H-NS protein, for "<b>H</b>istone-like <b>n</b>ucleoid <b>s</b>tructuring protein," is a nucleoid-associated multifunctional protein that is capable of condensing <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Dame RT, Wyman C, Goosen N,2000</a> and supercoiling DNA <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Zimmerman SB.,2006. Dame RT.,2005. McLeod SM, Johnson RC,2001. Tupper AE, Owen-Hughes TA, Ussery DW, Santos DS, Ferguson DJ, Sidebotham JM, Hinton JC, Higgins CF,1994</a>. It is a global transcriptional silencer of genes with high AT content <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Dorman CJ.,2004. Fang FC, Rimsky S,2008</a>, regulates 5% of all <i>Escherichia coli</i> genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hommais F,2001</a>, and plays a key role in global chromosome organization in bacteria <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wang W, Li GW, Chen C, Xie XS, Zhuang X,2011</a>. This protein acts as a pleiotropic transcriptional factor with a strong preference for horizontally acquired genes among the 250 loci to which it binds <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yamada H,1990. Oshima T, Ishikawa S, Kurokawa K, Aiba H, Ogasawara N,2006</a>. H-NS functions almost exclusively as a transcriptional repressor, although there is no clear evidence that this regulator is an activator. Currently, no inducer for this regulator has been reported in the literature, although Reush et al. proposed that this regulator can form a complex with a short chain of polyhydroxybutyrate <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Reusch RN, Shabalin O, Crumbaugh A, Wagner R, Schröder O, Wurm R,2002</a>. Additional genes might be identified by high-throughput analysis <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Uyar E, Kurokawa K, Yoshimura M, Ishikawa S, Ogasawara N, Oshima T,2009</a>.  H-NS suppresses transcription initiation at many intragenic sites as well as transcription of non-coding RNAs genome-wide <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Singh SS, Singh N, Bonocora RP, Fitzgerald DM, Wade JT, Grainger DC,2014</a>.  H-NS plays an important role in the regulation of many genes in response to environmental changes and adaptation to stress; therefore, it is  capable of controlling its own synthesis <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Falconi M,1993. Falconi M,1996</a>. It also regulates transcription of many other genes that participate in a variety of cellular functions, including genes involved in the following processes or responses: biogenesis of flagella <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Bertin P,1994. Soutourina O,1999. Landini P,2002</a>, transcription control of the type I fimbria structural genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Donato GM,1999. Donato GM,1997. Olsen PB,1998. Olsen PB,1994. Schembri MA,1998</a>, acid resistance  <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shin M,2005</a>, the functional glutamic acid-dependent system <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">De Biase D,1999</a>, osmotically inducible genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Bouvier J,1998</a>, the glutamate decarboxylase-dependent acid resistance system <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Giangrossi M,2005. Ma Z,2002. Tramonti A,2002. Hommais F,2001</a>, osmotic control <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lucht JM,1994. Rajkumari K,2001</a>, the type II secretion pathway <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Francetic O,2000</a>, carbon sources <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Rimsky S,1990</a>, genes involved in the RNA component of the small subunit (30S subunit) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Afflerbach H,1998. Gralla JD.,2005</a>, and proteases <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Forns N,2005</a>, among others. It has also been shown to be involved in survival during stationary phase, conferring to the bacteria a growth advantage during stationary phase (GASP) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chib S,2012</a>. A lack of H-NS results in overexpression of <i>ycdT</i>, <i>ydeH</i>, <i>yeal</i>, <i>yeaJ</i>, <i>yaiC</i>, <i>csgD</i>, <i>iraP</i>, and <i>ydiV</i>, while expression of <i>fliA</i> is decreased. These results indicate that H-NS influences motility via multiple regulatory linkages that include, minimally, the messenger molecule cyclic di-GMP, the biofilm regulatory protein CsgD, and the sigma factors &sigma;<sup>S</sup> and &sigma;<sup>F</sup>, thus impacting motility through diverse regulatory pathways <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kim EA, Blair DF,2015</a>.  H-NS is capable of inducing severe bends in the DNA, interacting with a large number of DNA regions that contain a planar curvature <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yamada H,1990. Yamada H, Yoshida T, Tanaka K, Sasakawa C, Mizuno T,1991. Jáuregui R, Abreu-Goodger C, Moreno-Hagelsieb G, Collado-Vides J, Merino E,2003</a>. It has been suggested that H-NS binds strongly to sites carrying a 10-bp AT-rich consensus sequence, which functions as a nucleation site for the formation of a repressive higher-order nucleoprotein complex <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lang B,2007. Sette M, Spurio R, Trotta E, Brandizi C, Brandi A, Pon CL, Barbato G, Boelens R, Gualerzi CO,2009</a>. H-NS binds to intergenic regions as well as regions within genes, but not all genes that bind H-NS are affected by this protein, a fact that is in agreement with the primary role assigned to HN-S in maintenance of nucleoid structure.There is a higher diversity in the sequence of HNS-bound intergenic regions than in HNS-unbound intergenic regions, and that diversity sometimes alters gene expression <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Higashi K,2016</a> The HNS-bound regions have been conserved through evolution in the <i>E. coli</i> lineage<a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Higashi K,2016</a> Currently, there are different models for the formation of DNA-H-NS-DNA bridges which show that this protein binds in tandem to sequences in the genome, forming multimers <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Dame RT.,2005. Dorman CJ,2007. Luijsterburg MS,2006</a>.  The intrinsic curvature and flexibility of binding sites of H-NS were investigated. The preference of H-NS for high intrinsic curvature is not as clear as with other DNA-binding proteins. The binding sites for H-NS show no preference to specific curvature level, and the binding affinity for H-NS does not seem to depend on the level of intrinsic curvature <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Liu G, Ma Q, Xu Y,2018</a>  As expected for a gene involved in the modulation of many cellular processes, the expression of <i>hns</i> is regulated by several systems and at different levels. At the transcription level, <i>hns</i> is autoregulated, and it is controlled by different transcription factors. <i>hns</i> is induced by high hydrostatic pressure <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Welch TJ, Farewell A, Neidhardt FC, Bartlett DH,1993</a> and DNA synthesis <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Free A, Dorman CJ,1995</a>. At the posttranscriptional level, it is subject to regulation by the sRNAs Hfq and DsrA <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lease RA, Belfort M,2000. Brescia CC, Mikulecky PJ, Feig AL, Sledjeski DD,2003. Majdalani N, Vanderpool CK, Gottesman S,2005. Brescia CC, Kaw MK, Sledjeski DD,2004. Repoila F, Majdalani N, Gottesman S,2003</a>.  It is a DNA-binding protein with similarity to StpA <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shi X, Bennett GN,1994. Zhang A, Belfort M,1992</a> and these two proteins can have similar functions <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Ali Azam T, Iwata A, Nishimura A, Ueda S, Ishihama A,1999. Azam TA,2000</a>. It has an approximately fivefold-lower affinity for DNA than StpA and has a major preference for curved DNA <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Sonnenfield JM, Burns CM, Higgins CF, Hinton JC,2001</a>.  StpA appears to be a backup for Hns in the regulation of a set of nonessential and recently acquired genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Srinivasan R,2013</a>.  Expression of <i>stpA</i> from a plasmid can complement an <i>hns</i> mutant phenotype and StpA is able to repress and activate a subset of H-NS-regulated genes, but the specific mechanisms remain to be determined <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shi X, Bennett GN,1994. Sonnenfield JM, Burns CM, Higgins CF, Hinton JC,2001. Sonden B,1996. Zhang A, Rimsky S, Reaban ME, Buc H, Belfort M,1996. Uyar E, Kurokawa K, Yoshimura M, Ishikawa S, Ogasawara N, Oshima T,2009. Sonden B,1996</a>. A dominant negative form of StpA can disrupt H-NS activity and vice versa, and H-NS can interact with StpA at two distinct domains to form heterodimers in vitro; also, there is evidence that these proteins can form homodimers <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Johansson J, Eriksson S, Sondén B, Wai SN, Uhlin BE,2001. Williams RM, Rimsky S, Buc H,1996. Dorman CJ, Hinton JC, Free A,1999. Williams RM, Rimsky S, Buc H,1996</a>. For this reason, in the absence of H-NS the StpA protein is rapidly degraded in a Lon protease-dependent manner <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Johansson J, Eriksson S, Sondén B, Wai SN, Uhlin BE,2001. Johansson J, Uhlin BE,1999</a>; protection from proteolytic degradation appears to be mediated by a direct interaction between StpA and H-NS <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Johansson J, Eriksson S, Sondén B, Wai SN, Uhlin BE,2001</a>. On the other hand H-NS also may form heterotrimeric complexes with Hha and YdgT <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Madrid C,2007. Paytubi S,2004</a>. It was proposed that Hha enhances the oligomerization of Hns/StpA which is necessary to form DNA-protein filaments to repress transcription <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Ueda T, Takahashi H, Uyar E, Ishikawa S, Ogasawara N, Oshima T,2013. Renault M, García J, Cordeiro TN, Baldus M, Pons M,2013</a>. The high-resolution structure based on solid-state NMR spectra from full-length H-NS has been determined <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Renault M, García J, Cordeiro TN, Baldus M, Pons M,2013</a>.   H-NS is a small protein and it is an abundant nucleic acid protein in the genome, with about 20,000 copies per cell. This regulator belongs to the histone-like family of transcriptional regulators, and the structure of the protein consists of two structured domains which are separated by a flexible linker <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Dorman CJ.,2004</a>.  The linker region connecting the N-terminal oligomerization and C-terminal DNA-binding domains  promotes H-NS/DNA binding and thus plays an important role in H-NS gene silencing. H-NS does not play a major role in chromosomal compaction <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gao Y, Foo YH, Winardhi RS, Tang Q, Yan J, Kenney LJ,2017</a> The N-terminal domain is required for oligomerization and it  is involved in protein-protein interactions, while the purified C-terminal domain is involved in DNA binding <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Dorman CJ.,2004. Dorman CJ, Hinton JC, Free A,1999. Williams RM, Rimsky S, Buc H,1996. Rimsky S,2004. Sette M, Spurio R, Trotta E, Brandizi C, Brandi A, Pon CL, Barbato G, Boelens R, Gualerzi CO,2009</a>. The cooperative binding of H-NS to DNA is related to protein-protein interactions <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Giangrossi M, Wintraecken K, Spurio R, de Vries R,2014</a>. The N-terminal domain is composed of two dimerization sites, dimerization site-1 and site-2, which are both required for H-NS oligomerization. The dimerization site-2 of H-NS is required for gene silencing and nucleoprotein filament formation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yamanaka Y, Winardhi RS, Yamauchi E, Nishiyama SI, Sowa Y, Yan J, Kawagishi I, Ishihama A, Yamamoto K,2018</a>  H-NS forms two compact clusters associated with each copy of the chromosome. These clusters are located near the one-quarter and three-quarter positions along the long axis of the cell <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wang W, Li GW, Chen C, Xie XS, Zhuang X,2011</a>. In cells with three clusters, the additional cluster tends to appear in the middle <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wang W, Li GW, Chen C, Xie XS, Zhuang X,2011</a>. The two cluster formations are induced by the N-terminal domain-driven oligomerization of the protein <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wang W, Li GW, Chen C, Xie XS, Zhuang X,2011</a>. H-NS sequesters the regulated genes and operons into these clusters and juxtaposed numerous DNA segments broadly distributed throughout the chromosome <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wang W, Li GW, Chen C, Xie XS, Zhuang X,2011</a>. Wang et al. (2011) reported that H-NS clusters could thus serve as anchoring points for numerous DNA loci distributed throughout the genome, creating DNA loops connecting the anchored loci <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wang W, Li GW, Chen C, Xie XS, Zhuang X,2011</a>.   It was found that the promoter islands, which are regions of DNA containing multiple transcription start sites, are recognized and inactivated by HNS. These promoter islands are associated with genes acquired horizontally <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Panyukov VV, Ozoline ON,2013</a>.  Based on ChIP-chip and chip-seq analysis, it was proved that Rho-dependent termination abrogates the H-NS-DNA interactions, leading to transcriptional derepression of H-NS-silenced genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chandraprakash D, Seshasayee AS,2014</a>.  An <i>hns</i>-<i>stpA</i> double mutant displayed slow growth and resulted in high expression of otherwise-silent horizontally acquired genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Srinivasan R, Scolari VF, Lagomarsino MC, Seshasayee AS,2015</a>. H-NS engineered mutants showed enhanced acid tolerance <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gao X, Yang X, Li J, Zhang Y, Chen P, Lin Z,2018</a>  Several mutations in the &sigma;<sup>38</sup> (RpoS) factor, as well as a duplication of ~40% of the genome centered around the origin of replication, converge in partially redressing the transcriptional imbalance of a strain lacking the H-NS/StpA gene-silencing system. The above finding suggests a possible role for &sigma;<sup>38</sup> in transcription of many H-NS targets <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Srinivasan R, Scolari VF, Lagomarsino MC, Seshasayee AS,2015</a>.  Rangarajan and Schnetz showed that when transcription from a neighboring region extends into an H-NS-bound locus, it can disrupt local H-NS repression in a dose-dependent manner, indicating a novel mechanism by which H-NS-mediated silencing can be reversed <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wade JT, Grainger DC,2018</a>  Based on proteomic studies, it was determined that the activities of nucleoid-associated proteins (NAPs) such as  H-NS, HU, IHF, and FIS are also modulated by posttranslational modifications (PTMs) that signal differences in growth conditions; because none of the NAPs are known to bind any signaling ligands, these growth condition signals may be compensated for by these PTMs <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Dilweg IW, Dame RT,2018</a>  The negative supercoiling of DNA in Fis, H-NS, and HU transcription factors modulates their 3D shape and determines their molecular dynamics <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Japaridze A, Muskhelishvili G, Benedetti F, Gavriilidou AF, Zenobi R, De Los Rios P, Longo G, Dietler G,2017</a> The circular supercoiled DNA dynamically folds in particular higher-order structures (hyperplectonemes) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Japaridze A, Muskhelishvili G, Benedetti F, Gavriilidou AF, Zenobi R, De Los Rios P, Longo G, Dietler G,2017</a>  Reviews: <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Luijsterburg MS,2006. Dorman CJ,2007. Dame RT.,2005. Williams RM,1997. Dorman CJ.,2004. Repoila F, Majdalani N, Gottesman S,2003. Navarre WW,2016. Grainger DC,2016. Winardhi RS, Yan J, Kenney LJ,2015</a>.			ECK12	Active	
ECK125269264	ECK120011240	Mlc	DgsA, better known as Mlc, "<b>m</b>akes <b>l</b>arge <b>c</b>olonies," <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hosono K, Kakuda H, Ichihara S,1995</a>is a transcriptional dual regulator that controls the expression of a number of genes encoding enzymes of the <i>Escherichia coli</i> phosphotransferase (PTS) and phosphoenolpyruvate (PEP) systems <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Plumbridge J,2002. Kimata K,1998</a>. In addition, Mlc regulates expression of the MalT transcriptional regulator, the activator of the maltose regulon <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Decker K,1998</a>.   Mlc repressor function is disabled by binding of Mlc to an actively-transported and dephosphorylated form of PtsG (EIICB<sup>Glc</sup>) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lee SJ,2000. Schiefner A, Gerber K, Seitz S, Welte W, Diederichs K, Boos W,2005</a>to 2.85 and 2.7 &Aring; resolution and Mlc in complex with four molecules of enzyme IIBGlc (EIIB) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Nam TW, Jung HI, An YJ, Park YH, Lee SH, Seok YJ, Cha SS,2008</a> to 2.85 &Aring; resolution. Mlc forms stable dimers and binds to palindromic operator sites. The N-terminal region has a helix-turn-helix domain, and a C-terminal helix is implicated in EIICB<sup>Glc</sup> binding <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Seitz S, Lee SJ, Pennetier C, Boos W, Plumbridge J,2003</a>. Shin D,2001</a> but it is also repressed and activated by CRP <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shin D,2001</a> Mlc binds to sites with a length of 23 bp. Its consensus sequence has been determined <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Plumbridge J.,2001</a> Mlc and VC2007, an ortholog of Mlc in <i>Vibrio cholerae</i>, bind to the same two operator sites in the <i>ptsG</i> upstream regulatory region. Cho S,2005</a> At the post-transcriptional level, Mlc interacts with MtfA, which is involved in the regulation of the <i>ptsG</i> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Becker AK,2006</a>  Mlc is a member of the ROK (<b>r</b>epressor, <b>O</b>RFs, <b>k</b>inases) (NagC/XylR) family of proteins, which contains at least two distinct classes of proteins: xylose repressor (XylR) and a series of glucose/fructose kinases <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Titgemeyer F, Reizer J, Reizer A, Saier MH Jr,1994. ECK125269265	ECK120011492	PhoP-Phosphorylated	PhoP is a dual transcriptional regulator that is activated in response to low extracellular levels of divalent cations, e.g., magnesium or calcium <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Groisman EA,1992. In <i>Escherichia coli</i> K-12, PhoP activates transcription of a large collection of genes involved in Mg<sup>2+</sup> homeostasis, resistance to antimicrobial peptides, acid resistance, and LPS modification <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kato A,1999. At high levels of magnesium the phospho-PhoP phosphatase activity of the sensor protein is induced <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">10807931</a>.  Regulation of the PhoQ/PhoP system is connected to the EvgS/EvgA system through the small inner membrane protein B1500. The EvgS/EvgA system is involved in acid resistance and directly regulates expression of B1500. B1500 interacts with PhoQ and increases the level of PhoP phosphorylation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Eguchi Y,2007</a>.  PhoP-P binds to tandemly arranged binding sites known as PhoP boxes, consisting of two direct repeats separated by 5 nucleotides <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kato A,1999</a>. ECK125269266	ECK120012802	MetR	MetR participates in controlling several genes involved in methionine biosynthesis <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Weissbach H,1991</a>and a gene involved in protection against nitric oxide <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Membrillo-Hernandez J,1998</a>  Homocysteine, an intermediate in the biosynthesis of methionine <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Urbanowski ML,1989</a> binds to MetR and enhances the activity of some MetR-activated promoters (i.e., metE and glyA)  by enhancing  the affinity in DNA-binding sites <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lorenz E,1996. Weissbach H,1991</a> But homocysteine is also able to decrease the activity of other promoters that are activated (i.e., metH, metA, and hmp)  or repressed (i.e., metR) by MetR <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Urbanowski ML,1989. Cai XY,1989. Weissbach H,1991</a> The <i>glyA</i> and <i>hmp</i> genes are transcribed divergently  and have  a common regulatory region that contains two MetR DNA-binding sites. When homocysteine is present, MetR binds to two MetR sites and activates <i>glyA</i> and represses <i>hmp</i> transcription, but in the absence of homocysteine MetR binds only one site and activates <i>hmp</i> and represses <i>glyA</i> transcription <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Membrillo-Hernandez J,1998</a>  MetR appears to first bind DNA and then homocysteine causes the effect on transcription <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Cai XY,1989</a> The nitrosated compound <i>S</i>-nitrosoglutathione (GSNO) removes homocysteine by  nitrosation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Membrillo-Hernandez J,1998</a>   The RNA polymerase and MetR interact for transcription activation, but distinct residues of RNA polymerase are involved in different promoters because the MetR-binding sites are located at different positions <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Fritsch PS, Urbanowski ML, Stauffer GV,2000. Lorenz E,1996</a>. MetR, as a dimer <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Cai XY,1989</a>  recognizes and binds a DNA sequence with dyad symmetry <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Cai XY,1989. ECK125269267	ECK120012313	YdeO	YdeO belongs to the AraC/XylS family of transcriptional regulators and shows more similarity to YhiW, AppY, AdiY, and GadX than the other AraC/XylS regulators <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gallegos MT,1997. Ibarra JA, Pérez-Rueda E, Segovia L, Puente JL,2008</a>. The members of this family exhibit two domains, an amino-terminal domain involved in coinducer recognition and dimerization and a carboxy-terminal domain that contains a potential helix-turn-helix DNA-binding motif <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gallegos MT,1997. Martin RG, Rosner JL,2001</a>.  YdeO activates genes involved in the cellular response to acid resistance <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Masuda N,2002. Masuda N,2003. Ma Z,2004</a>. Masuda N,2003. Ma Z,2004</a>. Several of the genes directly regulated by YdeO are also directly regulated by GadX <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Masuda N,2003</a>.  The YdeO regulon has been determined <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yamanaka Y,2014</a>. YdeO plays an important role in survival under both acidic and anaerobic conditions <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yamanaka Y,2014</a>.  Based on transcriptome analysis, the YdeO -> GadE transcriptional cascade was determined. A total of 106 genes were affected by YdeO, among which 53 genes were upregulated, and 23 of these were induced by GadE; a model has been proposed <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yamanaka Y,2014</a>.  The YdeO-box is an inverted repeat of hexanucleotides, with the sequence 5´-ATTTCA-3´, and the length of the spacer between this inverted repeat sequence ranges from 9 to 21 nucleotides <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yamanaka Y,2014</a>.  <i>ydeO</i> is transcribed in an operon together with the gene <i>safA</i> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Masuda N,2003</a>. This gene codes for a protein that directly interacts with PhoQ to activate the PhoQ/PhoP system, which regulates genes, including Mg<sup>2+</sup> transporters and lipopolysaccharide modification genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Eguchi Y,2007</a>.  YdeO expression is induced by UV irradiation via the EvgS/EvgA system and thus controls the expression of 21 genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yamanaka Y, Ishihama A, Yamamoto K,2012</a>. Gunsalus RP,1994</a> It acts primarily as a negative transcriptional regulator under anaerobic conditions. approximately 2-fold above that of the wild-type strain <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Toya Y, Nakahigashi K, Tomita M, Shimizu K,2012</a>  An exhaustive analysis of the ArcA response regulator was carried out, based on different experimental methodologies, High-throughputs (ChIP-chip and ChIP-seq), bioinformatic analysis, DNase I footprinting, and gene expression profiling, and the results showed that ArcA is a global repressor of carbon oxidation pathways <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Park DM,2013</a> ArcA-P directly regulates the expression of 85 operons under anaerobic growth shown that most ArcA-binding sites contain additional direct repeat (DR) elements as well as the two required for binding an ArcA dimer, and this finding provided a  novel insight into how ArcA accomplishes global regulation of gene expression <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Park DM,2013</a> More than 50% of the sites have additional DR elements, and the majority of ArcA-binding sites contain three to five DR elements, predominantly at a CTC spacing of 11 bp <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Park DM,2013</a> The variations in the number, spacing, location, and predicted strength of DR elements within the chromosomal ArcA-binding regions suggest plasticity in the architecture of ArcA-binding sites for either repressed or activated operons <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Park DM,2013</a>  The growth and the net final biomass produced in an <i>arcA</i> single deletion mutant strain growing under anaerobic fermentation conditions were affected relative to responses of the Hamilton R, Laird MW,2018</a>  ArcA promotes dehydration tolerance, both independently and through ArcZ or RpoS <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chen AI, Goulian M,2018</a>  Deletion of global Martin RG,2002. Martin RG, Rosner JL,2003. Pomposiello PJ,2001</a> the <i>marA/soxS/rob</i> regulon, involved in antibiotic resistance <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,1996. Griffith KL, Becker SM, Wolf RE Jr,2005. Ariza RR,1995</a> superoxide resistance <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Jair KW,1996. Nunoshiba T,1992. Wu J, Weiss B,1992</a> and tolerance to organic solvents <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">White DG, Goldman JD, Demple B, Levy SB,1997. Aono R,1998</a>and heavy metals <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Nakajima H, Kobayashi K, Kobayashi M, Asako H, Aono R,1995</a> The activity of each protein is induced by different signals: the activity of Rob is increased with dipyridyl, bile salts, or decanoate <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Rosner JL, Dangi B, Gronenborn AM, Martin RG,2002. Rosenberg EY,2003</a> and the activities of MarA and SoxS are increased by the aromatic weak acid salicylate <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Pomposiello PJ,2001</a>CITS:[27098660]|and by oxidative stress <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Demple B,1996</a> respectively. SoxS was induced by tellurite (TeO<sub>3</sub><sup>2-</sup>) in a DNA microarray analysis  <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Molina-Quiroz RC, Loyola DE, Díaz-Vásquez WA, Arenas FA, Urzúa U, Pérez-Donoso JM, Vásquez CC,2014</a>  Many genes are regulated by all three proteins; however, some genes are regulated by only one of them. The differential regulation of these genes might be caused by the degeneracy of their DNA-binding sites <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Pomposiello PJ,2003</a>  SoxS, MarA, and Rob bind as monomers to the same DNA site, a degenerate 20-bp sequence known as the Mar-Sox-Rob box, which has to be in a specific orientation and distance relative to the  -35 and  -10 boxes of the promoter <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,1999. Wood TI,1999</a> In class I promoters, the activators bind upstream of the  -35 box and are generally oriented in the backward direction, while in class II promoters the proteins overlap the  -35 promoter hexamer and generally are oriented in the forward direction <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,1999. Wood TI,1999</a> As a subset of the class I promoters, the Mar-Sox-Rob box is separated by ~30 bp from the  -10 hexamer but can be functional in either orientation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,1999. Wood TI,1999</a>  The Mar-Sox-Rob box contains an "invariant A" at position 1, two recognition elements, the RE1 at position 4-7 and RE2 at position 15-18, and a 7-bp A/T-rich spacer separating these elements <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kwon HJ,2000. Dangi B,2001. Griffith KL,2001</a> There are more than 10,000 such binding sites per genome <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Griffith KL, Shah IM, Myers TE, O'Neill MC, Wolf RE Jr,2002</a> However, the majority of these sites are not functional because they are not in the proper orientation or distance relative to the promoter <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG, Gillette WK, Martin NI, Rosner JL,2002</a> It was proposed that these proteins bind to their target sites by a mechanism named "DNA scanning" or "prerecruitment." In prerecruitment, the protein first binds to RNA polymerase in solution, and the binary complex then scans the DNA to find its binding sites <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG, Gillette WK, Martin NI, Rosner JL,2002. Griffith KL, Shah IM, Myers TE, O'Neill MC, Wolf RE Jr,2002</a> It has been shown that SoxS binds to the C-terminal domain of the &alpha;-subunit of RNA polymerase (&alpha;-CTD), in particular, it interacts with amino acid residues lying in or near the DNA-binding determinant of &alpha;-CTD. Apparently, SoxS functions as a co-sigma factor and redirects RNA polymerase to promoters carrying a SoxS-binding site <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shah IM, Wolf RE Jr,2004</a>   A new interaction that also occurs via prerecruitment between &sigma;<sup>70</sup> R4 of RNA polymerase (RNAP) and SoxS has been identified <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Zafar MA, Shah IM, Wolf RE Jr,2010</a>. There is evidence for the existence of two types of interactions between SoxS and &sigma;<sup>70</sup> R4, with one type being able to occur "off-DNA" (within a SoxS-RNAP binary complex that scans the chromosome for SoxS-dependent promoters) and with the other one occurring "on-DNA" (within a SoxS-RNAP binary complex residing at a SoxS-dependent promoter) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Zafar MA, Shah IM, Wolf RE Jr,2010</a>. SoxS is now the first example of an <i>Escherichia coli</i> transcriptional activator that uses a single positive control surface to make specific protein-protein contacts with two different subunits of RNAP <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Zafar MA, Shah IM, Wolf RE Jr,2010</a>. In class I promoters, the &sigma;<sup>70</sup> R4 region binds to the -35 box and SoxS binds upstream of the hexamer, but in class II promoters SoxS binds to the -35 box,  occluding the binding of &sigma;<sup>70</sup> R4 <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Zafar MA, Sanchez-Alberola N, Wolf RE Jr,2011</a>  SoxS, MarA, and Rob belong to the AraC/XylS family of transcriptional regulators <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gallegos MT,1997</a>and, like other members of this family, they have two helix-turn-helix (HTH) motifs for DNA binding: one of them, located in the N-terminal region, interacts with the element RE1 of the Mar-Sox-Rob box, and the HTH located in the C-terminal region interacts with the element RE2 <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Griffith KL, Wolf RE Jr,2002. Rhee S, Martin RG, Rosner JL, Davies DR,1998. Dangi B,2001</a>  <i>soxS</i> is divergently transcribed from <i>soxR</i> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wu J,1991</a> SoxR is the transcriptional activator for <i>soxS</i> and a sensor for superoxide and nitric oxide through its [2Fe-2S] center. It activates <i>soxS</i> transcription when oxidized <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gaudu P, Weiss B,1996. Hidalgo E, Ding H, Demple B,1997</a> <i>soxS</i> is regulated only by its intracellular concentration. Upon removal of the inducer, SoxS is degraded by the Lon protease <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Griffith KL, Shah IM, Wolf RE Jr,2004</a> The binding of SoxS with DNA or RNA polymerase protects it from degradation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shah IM, Wolf RE Jr,2006</a>  Transcriptomic and statistical modeling analyses with Zn concentration shifts showed a transient change in the activity of the iron regulator Fur and of the oxidative stress regulator SoxS; these findings suggest a competitive binding mechanism for the physiology of Zn and Fe uptake <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Graham AI,2012</a>  Cadmium prevented the induction of <i>soxRS</i>-controlled protective enzymes by the redox-cycling agent paraquat <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Thomas M, Benov L,2018</a>, although this appeared not to be due to specific inactivation of the <i>soxRS</i> regulon and most probably resulted from generalized suppression of protein synthesis <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wang A, Crowley DE,2005. Thomas M, Benov L,2018</a>.  Reviews: <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Poole RK,2005. Touati D,2000. Cabiscol E, Tamarit J, Ros J,2000. Demple B,1996</a>  			ECK12	Active

ECK125269272	ECK120013342	CsgD	The protein CsgD, for "<b>C</b>urlin <b>s</b>ubunit <b>g</b>ene <b>D</b>," is a transcriptional regulator <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hammar M,1995</a>that regulates a number of genes involved in the Curli assembly, transport, and structural components <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Loferer H, Hammar M, Normark S,1997. Hammar M,1995</a> which are important for biofilm formation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Brombacher E,2003</a> In addition, it also regulates genes related to cell surface-associated structures <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Brombacher E,2003. Gualdi L,2007</a> In general the environmental conditions, such as low osmolarity, low growth temperature (<32&deg;C), and low growth, affect the expression of <i>csgD</i> and the production of the biofilm and cellulose <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gualdi L,2008. Prigent-Combaret C,2001. Olsén A, Arnqvist A, Hammar M, Normark S,1993</a>  Since <i>csgD</i> is induced during the mid-exponential phase of growth and the CsgD-dependent activation of <i>csg</i> genes is detected in the stationary phase, it has been suggested that CsgD is posttranscriptionally activated in the stationary phase  <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Brombacher E,2003</a>  CsgD might activate transcription through two different mechanisms, as suggested by the different locations of its binding sites in the regulatory regions of the genes it activates. When the genes <i>csgD</i> or <i>ompR</i> are overexpressed, the curli number is increased, with higher levels in cells overexpressing the <i>csgD</i> gene, and there is not an additive effect on the curli number or structure in a strain overexpressing both the <i>csgD</i> and <i>ompR</i> genes. In spite of the increase in the number of curli, there is no correlation with cell adhesion capability. Additionally, in strains overexpressing <i>csgD</i>, <i>ompR</i>, or both, the growth rate is decreased <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Perni S, Preedy EC, Landini P, Prokopovich P,2016</a>  DnaK promotes the correct folding of CsgD through the DnaK-DnaJ-GrpR chaperone system <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Sugimoto S,2018</a>			ECK12	Active	
ECK125269273	ECK120011412	MarA	MarA, "<b>m</b>ultiple <b>a</b>ntibiotic <b>r</b>esistance" <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Cohen SP,1993</a> participates in controlling several genes involved in resistance to antibiotics, oxidative stress <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Alekshun MN, Levy SB,1999</a> organic solvents <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">White DG, Goldman JD, Demple B, Levy SB,1997. Alekshun MN, Levy SB,1999. Asako H, Nakajima H, Kobayashi K, Kobayashi M, Aono R,1997</a> and heavy metals <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Alekshun MN, Levy SB,1999</a>  MarA, SoxS, and Rob are paralogous transcriptional regulators that show  45%  amino acid identity between them <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Cohen SP,1993</a>  the crystal structures for Rob <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kwon HJ,2000</a>and MarA <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Rhee S, Martin RG, Rosner JL, Davies DR,1998</a>confirm this similarity between them. They activate a common set of  genes,  but the expression and activity of each one of these proteins are induced by different signals: the activity of  Rob is increased with dipyridyl, bile salts, or decanoate <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Rosner JL, Dangi B, Gronenborn AM, Martin RG,2002. Rosenberg EY,2003</a>, and  the transcription of MarA and SoxS is increased by the aromatic weak acid  salicylate <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG, Gillette WK, Martin NI, Rosner JL,2002</a>and oxidative stress <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Demple B,1996</a> respectively.  Many genes are regulated by all three proteins; however, some genes are regulated only by one of them.  The differential regulation of these genes might be caused by the degeneracy of their DNA-binding sites <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Pomposiello PJ,2003</a>  These three monomer proteins bind to the same DNA site, a degenerate 19-bp sequence known as the sox-mar-rob box, which has to be in a specific orientation and distance  relative to the -35 and -10 boxes of the promoter <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,1999. Wood TI,1999</a>  In class I promoters, the activators bind upstream of the -35 box and are generally oriented in a backwards direction, while in class II promoters the proteins overlap the -35 promoter hexamer and generally are oriented in the forward direction <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,1999. Wood TI,1999</a> In a subset of the class I promoters the sox-mar-rob box is separated by ~30 bp from the -10 hexamer but can be functional in either orientation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,1999. Wood TI,1999</a>  For MarA it was shown that the extent of activation at different promoters is only poorly correlated with the strength of MarA binding to the specific mar box <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG, Bartlett ES, Rosner JL, Wall ME,2008</a> By using a computational model of transcriptional activation it was proposed that at the class I promoter of <i>mar</i> MarA increases the binding of RNA polymerase but not the occupancy. At the class II promoters of <i>sodA</i> and <i>micF</i> MarA can even decrease both RNA polymerase affinity and occupancy at the promoter. The model predicts that MarA increases the rate of transcription initiation while decreasing the overall presence of the transcription machinery at the promoter <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Wall ME, Markowitz DA, Rosner JL, Martin RG,2009</a>  The sox-mar-rob box contains an "invariant A" at position 1,  two recognition elements, the RE1 at postion 4-7 and RE2 at position 15-18, and a 7-bp  A/T-rich spacer separating these elements <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kwon HJ,2000. Dangi B,2001. Griffith KL,2001</a>. There are more than 10,000 such binding sites per genome <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Griffith KL, Shah IM, Myers TE, O'Neill MC, Wolf RE Jr,2002</a> However, the majority of these sites are not functional because they are not in the proper orientation or distance relative to the promoter <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG, Gillette WK, Martin NI, Rosner JL,2002</a> Based on these findings, it was proposed that these proteins activate the transcription by a mechanism named "DNA scanning" or "prerecruitment," which consists of the formation of the complex  RNA polymerase transcriptional regulator in the absence of DNA, and then this complex scans the DNA to bind to appropriate sites <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG, Gillette WK, Martin NI, Rosner JL,2002. Griffith KL, Shah IM, Myers TE, O'Neill MC, Wolf RE Jr,2002</a>  These three proteins belong to the AraC/XylS family of transcriptional regulators <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gallegos MT,1997</a>  and as with other members of this family they have two helix-turn-helix  (HTH) motifs for DNA binding, one of them, located in the N-terminal region, interacts with the element RE1 of the mar box, and the HTH located in the C-terminal region interacts with the element RE2 <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Griffith KL, Wolf RE Jr,2002. Rhee S, Martin RG, Rosner JL, Davies DR,1998. Dangi B,2001</a> In the case of Rob, it appears that only one of two HTH motifs makes base-specific contact with DNA; this was observed for the micF promoter <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kwon HJ,2000</a>  Cells carrying a mutation in <i>marA</i> at a glutamic acid residue (E89A) were able to express higher resistance to superoxides than those harboring wild-type <i>marA</i> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,2011</a> Thus, MarA E89A acts more like SoxS than MarA in exhibiting greater activation and binding at the class I promoter <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martin RG,2011</a>  The MarA hydrophobic core distant from their binding site is affected by single-site substitutions, altering its extent and specificity of DNA binding <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Koulechova DA, Tripp KW, Horner G, Marqusee S,2015</a>  <i>marA</i> is the second gene of the <i>marRAB</i> operon, which encodes an autorepressor (MarR) and an autoactivator (MarA) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Cohen SP,1993</a>  MarR is inactivated by salicylate, and then the operon is induced. Upon removal of the inducer (salicylate), MarA is degraded  by the Lon protease <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Griffith KL, Shah IM, Wolf RE Jr,2004</a>  and the binding of MarA with DNA or RNA polymerase protects it from degradation  <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shah IM, Wolf RE Jr,2006</a> The transcription level of <i>marA</i> was increased in the <i>Ion</i> mutant and in the presence of sodium salicyclate (NaSal) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Bhaskarla C, Das M, Verma T, Kumar A, Mahadevan S, Nandi D,2016</a>  The <i>mar</i> response system presents a unique antagonistic architecture, harboring weak positive feedback through MarA and a strong negative effect through MarR <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Rodrigo G, Bajic D, Elola I, Poyatos JF,2016</a>.  The adaptability and plasticity of a regulatory network were determined by using an <i>in vivo</i> system that was developed to identify variants of helix-turn-helix transcription for the MarA transcriptional regulator <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shultzaberger RK, Maerkl SJ, Kirsch JF, Eisen MB,2012</a>  Based on single-cell experiments and computational modeling, it was shown that MarA has two qualitative classes of promoters: 1) the amplifying promoters, which generate diversity and have a high channel capacity at low levels of MarA, and 2) the filtering promoters, which have low noise and only transmit information when the MarA level is high <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Rossi NA, Dunlop MJ,2017</a>.  Based on RT-qPCR, it was shown that stress resistance genes (<i>rpoS</i>, <i>marA</i>, <i>ygfA</i>, and <i>relE</i>) and antibiotic resistance genes (ARGs), especially efflux genes, are upregulated in the viable but nonculturable (VBNC) state induced in pathogenic <i>Escherichia coli</i> strains by low-level chlorination exposure <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Lin H, Ye C, Chen S, Zhang S, Yu X,2017</a>  Based on CRISPRi and by engineering MarA, the active degradation of MarA was analyzed. MarAs degradation impacted both the rate of coordination and the maximum coordination that downstream genes can achieve <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Rossi NA, Mora T, Walczak AM, Dunlop MJ,2018</a>  Reviews: <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Alekshun MN, Levy SB,1999. ECK125269275	ECK125257196	OmpR-P	The response regulator OmpR, for "<b>O</b>uter <b>m</b>embrane <b>p</b>rotein <b>R</b>egulator," <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Taylor RK,1981. 7042694. 17628138</a> belongs to the two-component system EnvZ/OmpR <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">1700256. 1311295. 1618729. 7855421. Matsubara M,1999. 10947842. 11973328. 12077136. 12453214. 15979641. 16553882. 12453215</a> EnvZ is an osmosensor histidine kinase and is known to be a transmembrane protein composed of three domains: an external sensory domain (amino terminal), a cytoplasmic transmitter domain (carboxyl terminal), and a transmembrane hydrophobic central domain <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">2668953. 9070221. 15357641</a> The sensor domain responds to changes in extracellular osmolarity. EnvZ and OmpR comprise a signal transduction system where the signal generated by the periplasmic receptor affects through a protein-protein interaction the EnvZ cytoplasmic component, which is transmitted via autophosphorylation of histidine kinase to the response regulator OmpR <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">1657696. 1700256. 1311295. 1618729. 7855421. Matsubara M,1999. 10760160. 12453214. 15979641. 11934608. Qin L,2001. 12453215</a> OmpR is localized preferentially at the nucleoid edges and close to the cell membrane under low-salt and acidic conditions <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">26156621</a> OmpR is unusual among response regulators, because it can bind DNA in the absence of phosphorylation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">18195018</a> Phosphorylation is slow at acid pH, but a physical interaction between OmpR and EnvZ promote dimerization, resulting in an active OmpR<sub>2</sub> which binds to DNA with higher affinity <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chakraborty S,2017</a>  OmpR is required for acidification under acid and osmotic stress conditions. Its activation is noncanonical, involving a regulatory mechanism requiring the OmpR cognate kinase EnvZ but not OmpR phosphorylation. OmpR shows increased affinity to DNA at acidic pH <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chakraborty S,2017</a> Acidification occurs through repression of a different amino acid decarboxylation system, such as those encoded by the genes <i>cadA</i>, <i>cadB</i>, <i>gadB</i>, <i>gadA</i>, <i>speA</i>, <i>speD</i>, <i>speF</i>, and <i>ompF</i> <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chakraborty S,2017</a> Based on microarray and transciptome analyses, OmpR was identified as a global regulator of the bacterial response to acid and osmotic stress. It also plays an important role as a key regulator of bacterial virulence, growth, and metabolism, in addition to its role in regulating outer membrane proteins <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">30524381</a>  Phosphorylated OmpR controls the transcriptional expression of several genes and operons whose products are involved in adaptation to changes in osmolarity, such as major outer membrane porins <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">3157679. Nara F,1986. Mizuno T,1987. 1741624. 1769957. 1618729. 8876642. 11973328. Yoshida T,2006</a>, and it negatively regulates the expression of flagella <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Shin S,1995</a> promotes biofilm formation <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Prigent-Combaret C,2001</a> increases curli gene expression <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">9573197. Jubelin G,2005</a> increases expression of several drug exporter genes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hirakawa H,2003</a> and confers increased beta-lactam resistance <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hirakawa H,2003</a> among other effects <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Coyer J,1990. Yamamoto K,2000. Goh EB,2004. Guillier M,2008</a> When the genes <i>csgD</i> or <i>ompR</i> are overexpressed, the curli number is increased, with higher levels found in cells overexpressing the <i>csgD</i> gene, and there is not an additive effect on the curli number or structure in a strain overexpressing both the <i>csgD</i> and <i>ompR</i> genes. In spite of the increase in the number of curli, there is no correlation with cell adhesion capability. Additionally, in strains overexpressing <i>csgD</i>, <i>ompR</i>, or both, the growth rate is decreased <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Perni S, Preedy EC, Landini P, Prokopovich P,2016</a>   This protein consists of two functional domains, the N-terminal receiver domain, which is phosphorylated, and the C-terminal output domain, which binds to DNA and interacts with the &sigma;<sup>70</sup> subunit of RNA polymerase to activate transcription <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">1321117. 7565603. 7568033. 8989318. Martínez-Hackert E, Stock AM,1997. 9719640. 12077136. 15979641. 12077136. 15979641. 8989318</a> The output domain belongs to the winged helix-turn-helix family of transcription factors <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">8989318. Martínez-Hackert E, Stock AM,1997</a> Its activity is silenced by the receiver domain, and phosphorylation relieves inhibition.   The crystal structure of the DNA-binding domain has been determined at 1.95-Å resolution <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Martínez-Hackert E, Stock AM,1997</a>and also has been solved by NMR spectroscopy <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">18195018</a> In addition, Rhee et al. identified the DNA-binding properties of the C-terminal of OmpR based on chemical shift changes and analysis of amino acid substitutions and base pair substitutions <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">18195018</a>.  This regulator binds in tandem to sequences that are 20 nucleotides long and possess conserved motifs composed of two 10-bp direct repeat subsites; each dimer binds to one of these conserved sequences <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">1769957. 1321117. 8876642. 9719640. 11934608. Yoshida T,2006. Rampersaud A,1994. 9878429. 7592927</a>  A model of regulation that explains the differential expression of porin genes has been described by Mattison et al. (2002) and by Yoshida et al. (2006) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Mattison K,2002. Yoshida T,2006. 11934608</a>. The investigators showed that in this model OmpR-P binds differentially and cooperatively to regulatory regions of the genes <i>ompF</i> and <i>ompC</i><a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Yoshida T,2006. 9719640. Mattison K,2002. 11934608</a>. Under high-osmolarity conditions, <i>ompF</i> is repressed by DNA looping while the <i>ompC</i> gene is expressed, but under low-osmolarity conditions the effects on these genes are the reverse, i.e., <i>ompC</i> expression is repressed while <i>ompF</i> expression is increased <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">11934608. Yoshida T,2006. Mattison K,2002. 9719640</a> However, a potential mechanism for the phosphorylation-mediated activation of OmpR-regulated transcription follows the same pattern as with other members of the OmpR/PhoB family of response regulators (RRs) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">23399542</a>  The cross-regulation of OmpR by CpxA, ArcB, or acetyl phosphate results in phosphorylation of OmpR in response to different conditions <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">10947842. 18761686. Matsubara M,1999</a>  Although in both <i>Salmonella enterica</i> serovar Typhimurium and <i>Escherichia coli</i> the OmpR transcriptional dual regulator is identical, its expression is regulated differently in the two bacterial species, and there are very large differences in the composition of the OmpR regulon. In addition, the OmpR regulon evolved with different responses and roles in the two species during acid stress <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">24603618</a>  In a genome-scale study based on ChIP-exo, RNA-seq analysis, and DNA motif sequence identification, the OmpR regulon was characterized. A total of 37 genes in 24 transcription units (TUs) were identified to be under this regulons control during osmotic stress (NaCl treatment) <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Seo SW, Gao Y, Kim D, Szubin R, Yang J, Cho BK, Palsson BO,201</a>, involving regulation of mostly membrane-located products of the genes <i>narU</i>, <i>ompX</i>, and <i>nuoN</i>, which are involved in diverse biological processes <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Seo SW, Gao Y, Kim D, Szubin R, Yang J, Cho BK, Palsson BO,201</a>.   Based on the phylogenetic distribution of OmpR and its target gene orthologs, it has been determined that the OmpR regulon is conserved within <i>Proteobacteria</i>, i.e., OmpR is highly conserved across species, but genes in <i>Escherichia coli</i> K-12 MG1655 are not, which suggests that other species may have different regulatory networks to cope with osmotic stress <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Seo SW, Gao Y, Kim D, Szubin R, Yang J, Cho BK, Palsson BO,201</a>. ECK125269276	ECK120011302	OxyR	OxyR, "oxidative stress regulator," is the transcriptional dual regulator for the expression of antioxidant genes in response to oxidative stress, in particular, elevated levels of hydrogen peroxide. The OxyR regulon includes genes involved in peroxide metabolism, redox balance, and peroxide protection by, for example, manganese uptake <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Storz G, Tartaglia LA, Ames BN,1990. Zheng M,2001. Mongkolsuk S, Helmann JD,2002. Anjem A,2009</a> Moreover, OxyR activates the synthesis of the small, noncoding <i>oxyS</i> RNA. This allows OxyR to regulate as many as 40 additional gene products indirectly by affecting mRNA stability or translation efficiency <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Altuvia S,1997</a> OxyR acts as a repressor for its own synthesis in both the oxidized and reduced forms <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Christman MF,1989. Tao K,1991</a> In addition, expression of <i>oxyR</i> is positively regulated by cAMP-activated Crp protein during exponential growth and negatively regulated by RpoS when cells enter stationary phase <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Gonzalez-Flecha B,1997</a> Based on microarray assays, a new function as regulator of S-nitrosylation (a nitrosative stress regulon) under anaerobic conditions has been determined for OxyR <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Seth D,2012</a> and it is distinct from the OxyR regulon activated by oxidative stress <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Seth D,2012</a>  OxyR consists of 305 amino acid residues and is a member of the LysR family of bacterial transcription factors <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Christman MF,1989. Tao K,1989</a> It is a tetramer in solution <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kullik I, Stevens J, Toledano MB, Storz G,1995</a> OxyR consists of two domains, the N-terminal domain, carrying a helix-turn-helix motif, and the C-terminal regulatory domain. A flexible linker connects both domains. The C-terminal domain contains the determinants for oligomerization and two redox-active cysteines, Cys199 and Cys208 <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Kullik I, Stevens J, Toledano MB, Storz G,1995. Lee C, Lee SM, Mukhopadhyay P, Kim SJ, Lee SC, Ahn WS, Yu MH, Storz G, Ryu SE,2004. Zaim J, Kierzek AM,2003</a> OxyR functions as a reversible cellular redox switch. In the presence of 100 nM H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, activation occurs by the formation of an intramolecular disulfide bond between Cys199 and Cys208 <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Storz G,1990. Lee C, Lee SM, Mukhopadhyay P, Kim SJ, Lee SC, Ahn WS, Yu MH, Storz G, Ryu SE,2004</a> It was determined that concentrations of >2 &mu;M extracellular H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> trigger the intracellular OxyR stress response, and >5 &mu;M begins to impair cell growth in minimal medium <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Li X, Imlay JA,2018</a> Inactivation involves reduction of the disulfide bond by glutaredoxin 1 and glutathione <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Zheng M, Aslund F, Storz G,1998</a> Oxidation is significantly faster than reduction, allowing for transient activation in a reducing environment <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Aslund F, Zheng M, Beckwith J, Storz G,1999</a> OxyR is also activated under nitrosative stress; this reaction involves S-nitrosylation of OxyR <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Hausladen A, Privalle CT, Keng T, DeAngelo J, Stamler JS,1996</a> The crystal structures of the regulatory domain of OxyR in both its reduced and oxidized forms have been solved. Comparison of both structures reveals that oxidation is accompanied by a significant structural change in the regulatory domain <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Choi H, Kim S, Mukhopadhyay P, Cho S, Woo J, Storz G, Ryu SE,2001</a>  In systematic studies of oligomerization, it was shown that some members of the LysR family, like OxyR, interact with other members of the family to form heterodimers, but the physiological significance of this is unknown <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Knapp GS, Hu JC,2010</a>. OxyR can bind as a pair of dimers bending to the DNA <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Muraoka S, Okumura R, Ogawa N, Nonaka T, Miyashita K, Senda T,2003. Gusarov I, Nudler E,2012</a>   OxyR recognizes a motif comprised of ATAGnt elements spaced at 10-bp intervals <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Toledano MB,1994</a> The binding to DNA differs significantly for the oxidized and reduced forms <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Toledano MB,1994</a> Reduced OxyR binds DNA at two pairs of major grooves separated by one helical turn, while oxidized OxyR occupies four consecutive major grooves. When bound to DNA, oxidized OxyR stimulates transcription by interacting with the C-terminal domain of the &alpha;-subunit of RNA polymerase <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Tao K, Zou C, Fujita N, Ishihama A,1995</a>  Review: <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Chiang SM, Schellhorn HE,2012</a>. Gallegos MT, Michán C, Ramos JL,1993. Ma Z,2002</a>   Tramonti et al. speculated that GadW binds in tandem to two directed repeat sequences, in the same orientation, in the  intergenic regions to activate or repress the transcription of the genes regulated <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Tramonti A,2008</a> The binding targets for GadW consist of 21-nucleotide-long directed repeat sequences that possess conserved motifs, called the GAD box, which is proposed to be the binding site for GadW and GadX <a href="#myRef" class="linkBlue">Tucker DL,2003. Tucker DL,2003.
